Another big, interesting thing that I've observed with AI agents is that it fundamentally boils down to two things. This is true for almost any agentic company. Policies are just a markdown file, and how can you iterate on that markdown file to affect a business KPI? For support, it's the resolution rate or CSAT. All that matters is how you go from, say, a 30 to 40 percent resolution rate to 90 percent. How can you iteratively improve to get there? The same fundamentals apply for compliance, ITSM, ITSD, and everything. So, we are seeing customers, some of the biggest consumer companies in the US, piloting us for internal support, even compliance and etc. It's very interesting with AI agents. It fundamentally boils down to markdown and how you can iteratively improve markdown to move a KPI. Okay, so I want to change gears here a little bit and talk a little bit about the advice you would give to the people in this room, right? So, the people in this room are predominantly college students or young people recently out of college. If you were rewinding back to your time as a college student, is there any advice you would give that's maybe different from the advice that they might be hearing from their peers, or what is kind of the norm, you think? Yeah, I mean, a lot of people thought I was stupid when I rejected that job offer. I mean, it seemed so insane because... They were like, "You're turning down this great offer from a quant firm, why would you do that?" Yeah. It's just that for me and my co-founder, we just wanted to give it a shot. We wanted to see how high we could go. That's pretty much it, and that's the reason and the entire way that we built the company. Even when scaling, like denying acquisition offers from some of the biggest companies in the world, that's how we pretty much built the entire company. We just wanted to reach our potential and see if this is the best we can do, and push it. It's kind of more than me; it's my co-founder's mentality. He's fundamentally a zero-motivated-by-money person. So, yeah, that's where it came from. And I read Paul Graham's essay on how to do wealth. It essentially boils down to starting a company and having equity in something big. That's pretty much it, yeah. What did your parents think about that in those early days? Like, were they excited? You said you grew up in a very modest upbringing. Presumably, getting this high-paying job would have been life-changing for your family. How did that conversation go? You're like, "I'm moving to SF. I'm going to start this crazy thing." My dad was super mad, to be honest. It was kind of like a big fight at home. But it's fine. I mean, what can they really do? They can't force a kid to do something. But it was really a hard conversation. I actually showed them what YC is, showed them YC videos, and showed them the companies they fund. I showed them that even if I don't succeed in a year or two, I can just go back and do the job thing again. When you come from a middle-class or lower-middle-class family, there is just a lot of expectation to do things, especially when I got a really good job offer and my parents were so happy about it. It wasn't just my parents; even internally, I felt, "Am I doing something wrong?" But again, my entire philosophy has been to just take a shot and see if it works out or not. So, when you think about now how college students or young people can put themselves in a position to find great startup ideas, what are some of the lessons from your experience that you think apply to the people in this audience? The biggest mistake we made, even after getting into YC, is we worked on a lot of stupid ideas which didn't make any revenue or do anything for a long time. People just want to have a lot of ideas. I can just go into ChatGPT and get 10 ideas on what to do, right? It's never about the idea; it's about if somebody is willing to pay you money for it. I don't even think you need to care about the market, to be honest. Is somebody willing to pay real money if you solve the problem, for the value that you delivered? That's the strongest approach we took, and it took me a lot longer to realize. Right now, even for our new products that we build, we make sure that the customer can actually pay for it. We predict the amount they are going to pay and get a commitment from the customer, and then we go and build it. And why is it that charging for a product so early is useful? I think a lot of people in the audience might think, "Oh, I'm just a college student, no one's ever going to pay me for something I make. Right now, I should just get started for free," or something like that. Why does that not tend to work in your mind? If it's an important enough problem, people will pay, either with money or with time, I would say. I mean, social media networks are just time—they don't charge you any money. But in general, for any B2B company, if the problem is important enough, people should be willing to pay money for it. Otherwise, you're just solving a fake problem. So, you are also part of a new generation of Indian-origin founders who are building companies both here and in SF. How do you think about that? Do you think about that, or is there any particular framework you have for how to expand your company and take advantage of your background from India? I think that you should just stay close to customers wherever you are. But if you are doing anything closer to Gen AI and very research-based things, I strongly think SF is the place because the amount of access you get to researchers is insane. Almost all the innovation in this Gen AI field is getting driven out of the Bay Area alone, compared to India. But again, if your customer is primarily based out of India, you should be there. What do you think when you look forward to the future and think about all of the pivots your company has gone through to get to where it's at now? What do you think the next few years look like for your company? Do you feel like you've found the thing that's going to become really big, or do you see yourself continuing to evolve, and how are you evolving yourself? Yeah, I think we are moving in a great direction, and I'm very confident. The biggest bottleneck of every single enterprise AI deployment, regardless of support or any automation that they want, is this concept called the forward-deployed engineer. You need to have a bunch of them coming and sitting with the customers and configuring it. We're trying to build an AI forward-deployed engineer, and we're going to launch it soon. That is where I would say I'm very confident because whenever you want to make these policy changes, or whenever you want to just spin up a new dashboard, or go from 40 to 60 percent resolution, our AI forward-deployed engineer is going to join. He will be on Slack, join Google Meets, take all the notes, and do the changes automatically. So, I'm very confident in the direction we are moving and how we are going to tackle AI adoption in enterprises. The biggest bottleneck right now is the forward-deployed engineer, and we're going to take it over. Could you tell us a little bit about how your company actually runs internally using AI? Your product is obviously very AI-driven, but in terms of the company itself, what are the tools that your engineers and salespeople are using? What does that actually look like? It's funny because one of our values is "automate, automate, automate." So, we force people to use as much automation as possible. In general, the overall mission of the company is to automate all of the world's work. We are intentionally moving in that direction—a generic automation builder able to automate anything on top of us. Some examples are: you don't need to have a personal assistant. You just add it, and it opens Claran and schedules the meetings for you. Salespeople use it in very interesting ways, too. They pull transcripts from Gong to analyze what the biggest things are that are working against a specific competitor across multiple deals. So yeah, everybody is using it. That's the one thing I love about tools like Claude. It turned a lot of people into builders, and people are using it innovatively to drive specific insights that would have otherwise taken humans a lot of time to go through. Yeah, I mean, if coding agents didn't exist, how many engineers do you think would be at your company versus what it currently is? What would the company look like without all of that? I think we would need at least six to seven times more engineers compared to now. We're relatively a very small engineering team. It's a very strong talent density and a small engineering team. That's crazy, right? Like 7x the number of engineers you would need if you didn't have one tool, which probably costs you much less than seven times your engineering team. It's more than cost; it's just better without the context switching. It's better for you to own the thing and build the entire thing rather than having a lot of people working on it. You can just ship much faster, whereas context transfer actually kills a lot of things and slows down progress. So given that, I imagine the kinds of people you look to hire on your team also are very different from the average SaaS company ten years ago, or at least in some ways different. I'm curious how you would describe that. Are there things that you guys look for that are maybe different than what a big tech company—who doesn't use that many coding agents yet—might look for? Our interview process is specifically designed where we ask people to vibe code, and then we remove AI access and ask them to change the code. You actually have them vibe coding in your interview process? Yeah, we ask them to vibe code, and then we remove access to that tool and ask them to change the code without AI. It's intentional because we want people to understand the code as well and how it works. Again, this also keeps evolving as AI models keep getting better and better. Do you really need to know how the code works even if Claude does the entire thing? But that's where we landed. Generally, the things we look for are some sort of extraordinary ability and spikiness that we can relate to. For my co-founder and me, that was me having one of the highest job offers, and my co-founder being third in the entire IIT while also getting the highest-paying job offer in India at a quant firm. We try to look for very spiky indicators that only 0.1 percent of people would have, and that's how we generally go about hiring. So, you know, I think a lot of people might look at themselves and say, "Oh, I'm a technical person, I'm an engineer, but what do I know about business? I don't have a business background necessarily." How did you guys think about that? You and your co-founder are both computer scientists. I assume you didn't know business before you started this company. What has been your experience on whether that pre-exposure matters, or if you just kind of learn it as you go? I mean, there are a lot of people who will buy your product even if you don't have a business background. You just have to find the right buyer. Zepto is one of those companies—they didn't care if you didn't have a big sales team. DoorDash doesn't care about salespeople either. You just have to find your ICP. But I guess I mean more for you guys as founders. In your own founder ability, do you feel like it would have benefited you, or do you feel like your technical abilities allowed you to get somewhere, and that's what gave you the opportunities? I personally bias a lot more towards builders than sellers. This is a mistake I made while starting the company. Me and my co-founder had this huge debate because I thought sales was the most important thing in the company. I was so wrong in so many ways. If you take a look at all the successful AI companies, it's product. None of them are succeeding because of a sales team. Nobody uses Anthropic because they have the best sales team. I don't think Anthropic and OpenAI even pay salespeople commissions. That's how little they care about traditional sales. And I think with AI, product is the most important thing. How good is your product at delivering a lot of value to the customer in a short amount of time? If you can prove that, everything else should follow through. Yeah. Okay, we're almost at time here. I'm curious if you have any parting final thoughts or reflections. You've now been at this for two or three years, you're no longer in school, and you've been spending your time split between San Francisco and Bangalore. Are there things that you now realize about how the world works that maybe weren't obvious to you when you were getting started, and you can leave people with some parting advice? Yeah, the biggest thing is just getting started, trying to sell the thing, jumping in, and burning your boats. Burning the boats is a good way to start. It is only really valuable and things get really real if you burn the boats. That's when I really felt it. When the company was not working, my co-founder and I were thinking, "Oh my god, we rejected all these job offers. What are we going to do?" It actually forces you to make things work. It's not like you are truly, dangerously burning boats—if you lose a job, you will get a job again, it's not going to go anywhere. But in general, just going at it and doing things has a lot stronger value. Especially with AI, the cost of building things is so low. People should just build things and try to deliver as much value as they can to a very small set of customers, and see if they can pay them money. Awesome. Thanks so much for coming, Varun. Really appreciate it. Thank you.

Treasury Secretary Scott Bessent: Economic Security Is National Security | Keynote Remarks at RNEF

Ronald Reagan Presidential Foundation & Institute(1.2M) ·44:47 ·345 views · 2026-05-29
Scott Bessent (U.S. Treasury Secretary)
Treasury Secretary Scott Bessent outlines the Trump administration's "economic security is national security" doctrine, detailing the devastating impacts of the "Operation Economic Fury" financial blockade on Iran, alongside domestic policies aimed a...
TL;DR Treasury Secretary Scott Bessent outlines the Trump administration's "economic security is national security" doctrine, detailing the devastating impacts of the "Operation Economic Fury" financial blockade on Iran, alongside domestic policies aimed at reshoring critical...

TL;DR

Treasury Secretary Scott Bessent outlines the Trump administration's "economic security is national security" doctrine, detailing the devastating impacts of the "Operation Economic Fury" financial blockade on Iran, alongside domestic policies aimed at reshoring critical supply chains, implementing permanent tax cuts, and establishing Trump Savings Accounts for American children.

The "Economic Security is National Security" Doctrine

  • Shift from Consumption to Production: Bessent argues that for decades, America mistakenly prioritized cheap consumer imports over manufacturing resilience. He calls for a structural return to measuring economic health at the "factory gate" rather than the "checkout counter."
  • The True Cost of Offshoring:
      • Allowing key factories in states like Michigan, Ohio, and Pennsylvania to go dark degraded America's "industrial commons"—the ecosystem of skilled labor, tooling, and local logistics needed for rapid adaptation.
      • The COVID-19 pandemic and the war in Ukraine exposed the danger of "just-in-time" supply chains over "just-in-case" preparedness, revealing severe vulnerabilities in defense surge capacity, medical supplies, and advanced technologies.
  • Separating Trade from Geopolitics: Past administrations treated commercial trade and national security as distinct silos. Bessent asserts that relying on strategic rivals like China for critical inputs inevitably collapses these silos, endangering national sovereignty.

"Operation Economic Fury" and the Blockade of Iran

  • A Multi-Front Economic Blockade: In coordination with the U.S. Navy, the Treasury has instituted a severe financial and maritime blockade. The Navy has shut down Iran’s primary oil export facility on Kharg Island, reducing Iranian oil-on-the-water to historical lows.
  • Severe Domestic Crises in Iran: According to Bessent, "Operation Economic Fury" has brought Iran to the end of its financial tether:
      • Inflation in Iran has spiked past 200%, forcing the regime to issue food vouchers and shut down the internet.
      • Approximately 40% to 50% of Iranian military and police forces are currently unpaid or failing to report to work.
  • Asset and Cryptocurrency Seizures:
      • The Treasury has frozen extensive offshore accounts belonging to the Islamic Revolutionary Guard Corps (IRGC), which Bessent claims was embezzling $400 million to $500 million per month from the Iranian people.
      • The U.S. government has outright seized approximately $1 billion in IRGC-linked cryptocurrency wallets.
  • Diplomatic Red Lines: Bessent confirms that while Iran has engaged in negotiations for the first time, President Trump’s core "red lines" remain firm: full denuclearization, no uranium enrichment, free and open access to the Strait of Hormuz, and no short-term financial relief.

Trade Policy & Rebuilding Critical Industrial Capacity

  • America First Trade Memorandum: Signed on President Trump's first day back in office, this directive instructs the Treasury, Commerce, Homeland Security, and USTR to treat trade as an instrument of national strategy rather than a technocratic afterthought.
  • Strategic Tariffs and Section 232 Actions:
      • The administration declared a national emergency regarding foreign trade practices, implementing reciprocal tariff actions explicitly built around national security.
      • Section 232 executive actions were signed to limit foreign import dependencies on processed critical minerals and derivative products.
  • Maritime and Shipbuilding Dominance: To combat America's shrunken shipbuilding capacity, the administration established a new Office of Maritime Industrial Capacity at the National Security Council to restore domestic shipping and logistics resilience.
  • Pharmaceutical Sovereignty: President Trump signed an executive order to fill the strategic Active Pharmaceutical Ingredients (API) reserve, introducing parallel Section 232 investigations and tariffs on foreign patented pharmaceuticals to secure domestic medicine supplies.

Domestic Economic Strategy: Tax Permanence, Trump Accounts, and the Fed

  • Permanence in Tax Policy: Bessent highlights the "One Big Beautiful Bill," which permanently codifies the tax cuts and full business expensing from the Trump 1.0 Tax Cuts and Jobs Act, providing long-term market certainty.
  • The Trump Savings Accounts Initiative: Described by Bessent as the most significant government benefit for young people since the GI Bill, the initiative aims to build a new class of American shareholders:
      • Seed Funding: Every child born during President Trump's term receives a $1,000 seed investment from the Treasury, placed into low-cost index funds.
      • Tax-Free Growth: Families can contribute up to $5,000 annually, compounding tax-free until age 18 to be used for education, starting a business, or a home down payment.
      • Private Philanthropy: Philanthropists Michael and Susan Dell have pledged $6.25 billion directly to the program to fund accounts for children residing in the bottom 80% of economic ZIP codes.
  • A "New Day" at the Federal Reserve: Bessent praises newly confirmed Fed Chairman Kevin Warsh. He anticipates a return to central banking basics, highlighting Warsh's intent to eliminate "forward guidance" in favor of market-driven credibility and Fed accountability.
  • Strong Dollar Policy: Bessent clarifies that a strong dollar is not defined by daily screen prices, but by structural tax, trade, and energy policies that make the U.S. the safest and highest-yielding destination for global capital.

Notable Quotes

"Economic security is national security. For a nation that cannot manufacture, mine, ship, or refine its needs gradually cedes its strength and sovereignty to others." — Scott Bessent, U.S. Treasury Secretary
"In a crisis, the nation with a broad and skilled industrial base has options; the nation that has hollowed out that base only has excuses." — Scott Bessent, U.S. Treasury Secretary
"While America slept, our vulnerabilities grew, but under President Trump's leadership, we are alert to the risk we can no longer ignore and attuned to the responsibility we can no longer defer." — Scott Bessent, U.S. Treasury Secretary
Thank you very much. It's an honor to be here, and I'd like to thank the Ronald Reagan Presidential Foundation and Institute for inviting me to join you today. Of course, this year's forum carries special significance as we celebrate 250 years of the American story. But milestones of this magnitude demand more than ceremony. They ask something of us. They invite us to reflect not just on the creation of our country, but on its condition. And as we focus today on America's economic future, they compel us to confront and correct decisions that have diminished our sovereignty in recent decades. President Reagan himself understood that renewal begins with reflection. "One thing that has made our Republic great," he said, "is that we don't hide from our mistakes, we learn from them, then we go on and do things better than we did before, better than we did before." That is the spirit in which I want to speak with you today, with candor and with confidence in our capacity to deliver another 250 years of American economic leadership. The truth is that for too long, America has been asleep. We mistook comfort for strength, we substituted efficiency for resilience, and we used consumption as a measure of prosperity. We told ourselves that so long as goods were cheaper overseas, it did not matter whether factories went dark in Michigan, Ohio, or Pennsylvania. We assumed that supply chains would always function smoothly, that adversaries would always behave responsibly, and that the invisible hand would correct vulnerabilities that too few in public life had the courage to confront. And while we reassured ourselves with those assumptions, risk accumulated all around us. Somewhere along the way, we lost sight of a foundational principle that previous generations understood instinctively: economic security is national security. For a nation that cannot manufacture, mine, ship, or refine its needs gradually cedes its strength and sovereignty to others. That is a dangerous dependency for any country. It is an unacceptable one for the United States of America. Now, nations rarely cede their security in a single moment. More often, they drift into dependence through a series of politically expedient decisions. For our part, we made a series of mistakes—some bipartisan, others ideological, and many defended long after their cost became impossible to ignore. One was treating trade policy as though it existed apart from national strategy. We convinced ourselves that we could pursue commercial partnerships in one silo while managing security relationships in another, as if the world would honor these distinctions. But a nation that relies on a rival for critical inputs, finances the rise of countries that do not share our interests, and allows its productive base to erode while promising to defend the international order will find, sooner or later, that these two silos collapse into each other. It was as damaging as our willingness to extend strategic trust where it had been... not earned. China's accession to the WTO and the granting of permanent normal trade relations were sold to the American people as steps that would level markets and moderate behavior. Instead, we left our workers to compete against state-led subsidies, excess capacity, and practices that distort trade and undermine reciprocity. We assumed the rules-based system would discipline these behaviors, only to find that in too many sectors, it accommodated them. And in the name of efficiency, we began to celebrate "just in time" while neglecting "just in case". The pandemic did not create our brittle supply chains so much as expose them. Suddenly, the world's most advanced economy found itself partially dependent on foreign suppliers for semiconductors, large-capacity batteries, critical minerals, and medicines that we no longer produced here at home. Regrettably, our recovery from the pandemic did not bring reform under the prior administration. Today, the United States still imports most of its rare earth minerals that are essential to the technologies that will shape military and economic power in the 21st century. Only a small share of the active pharmaceutical ingredients that we use in the United States are made here. And American shipbuilding capacity, which once helped to secure victory in war and prosperity in peace, has shrunk to a fraction of global production. So when I say America has been asleep, I do not mean that no one warned us. In fact, many did. Workers warned us when their plants shuttered and communities decayed. Military planners warned us when supply chains narrowed. Manufacturers warned us about subsidized competitors and dumped products. Public health officials warned us about pharmaceutical dependencies. The warning lights were glaring all around us. But our political class preferred the comfort of old formulas: cheaper was always better, offshoring was inevitable, industrial policy was unfashionable, and strategic dependence was acceptable so long as the cost remained invisible. Beneath every one of those mistakes lay a more basic failure in our philosophy: in reducing economics to consumption, we forgot production. We measured abundance at the checkout counter rather than at the factory gate. We talked about GDP but not enough about its composition, and we prized low-cost inputs without first asking whether a nation can remain sovereign when it loses command over the things that matter most. A country cannot outsource its industrial commons, ignore strategic concentration, and expect to remain secure. For manufacturing is more than output on a balance sheet; it is a reservoir of practical capability: engineers and welders, tool and die makers, logistic networks, plant managers, and workers who know how to solve problems on the factory floor. When the ecosystem is strong, a country can adapt quickly. When it is hollowed out, adaptation becomes slower, more costly, and less certain. We saw this during COVID in the scramble for ventilators, masks, and other medical equipment. We saw it again as the war in Ukraine reminded the world that high-intensity conflict consumes weapons and munitions at a pace far beyond peacetime assumptions. Surge capacity cannot be conjured overnight; it must be built before a crisis arrives. And a country that allows its industrial base to shrink in the quiet years should not be surprised when it struggles to replenish stocks in the danger ones. In a crisis, the nation with a broad and skilled industrial base has options; the nation that has hollowed out that base only has excuses. America should never again put itself in a position where its security depends on supply chains it does not control, factories it no longer maintains, or skilled trades it failed to preserve. That is why the President's agenda matters. Reasonable people can debate the calibration of any particular instrument, but the central strategic insight is undoubtedly sound: trade policy, industrial capacity, and national security are inseparable. And to allow foreign dependencies to degrade any one of these domains is to allow them to define America's future. So, on his first day back in office, President Trump issued the America First Trade Policy memorandum, a sweeping directive to Treasury, Commerce, Homeland Security, USTR, and other agencies to stop treating trade as a technocratic afterthought instead of an instrument of national strategy. Last April, the President declared a national emergency tied to the cumulative effects of foreign trade practices and announced reciprocal tariff actions, framing them explicitly around national and economic security. Again, people can debate design, sequencing, and scope. Healthy democracies should debate those questions. But no one should miss the larger point: chronic, non-reciprocal agreements, industrial hollowing, and strategic import dependence are not questions of commerce alone; they are fissures in our country's foundation. A few weeks later, President Trump signed executive action on processed critical minerals and derivative products under Section 232, expressly linking import dependence to risk for national security, defense readiness, price stability, and economic resilience. Some have tried to weaponize critical minerals against us, but this administration is ensuring they will not succeed. On maritime strength, President Trump signed an executive order to restore America's maritime dominance, established a new Office of Maritime Industrial Capacity at the National Security Council, and called for a maritime action plan to rebuild US shipbuilding capabilities. This is what it looks like to connect industrial capacity, logistics resilience, workforce development, and national security in one strategic frame. Finally, on pharmaceuticals, President Trump signed an executive order to fill the strategic active pharmaceutical ingredients reserve so that America is never again caught flat-footed when access to critical drug components is at risk. Last month, the White House also highlighted further action to impose tariffs on patented pharmaceutical products while noting parallel Section 232 investigations involving personal protective equipment, medical consumable devices, and robotics. The strategic message is unmistakable: supply chain resilience in medicine will no longer be an afterthought. This broader reorientation is also visible in the administration's trade agenda for 2026, as we work to secure reciprocity, strengthen domestic production, and align America's external economic relationships with our core national interest. Taken together, these efforts reflect the broader principle that President Trump has articulated clearly: economic security is national security. But let me be direct about what that doctrine does not mean. It does not mean retreating from the world. On the contrary, it means engaging with it on stronger, fairer, and more sustainable terms. It does not mean severing ties indiscriminately. It means distinguishing healthy interdependence from dangerous overdependence. It does not mean rejecting efficiency. It means refusing to worship efficiency when efficiency leaves our nation exposed. And it does not mean treating allies and adversaries alike. It means bringing our trading relationships and our security relationships back into alignment by building supply chains with trusted partners where appropriate, rebuilding capacity at home where necessary, and ensuring that the economic architecture supporting American power strengthens American security. Of course, America still holds enormous advantages, among them the world's deepest capital markets, extraordinary innovators, abundant energy potential, leading universities, an unmatched entrepreneurial culture, and workers who can outbuild and outcompete anyone so long as the playing field is fair. All we needed was a government willing to declare that decline is a choice, and renewal can be one too. So yes, while America slept, our vulnerabilities grew. While America slept, too many leaders accepted fragile supply chains as the price of modern life. While America slept, we let industrial policy erode and Main Street decay. We tolerated unfairness in the name of stability and conflated market access with strategic success. But America has awakened. We are alert to the fact that a resilient economy is the foundation of a strong Republic, that we measure the health of our economy not merely by what it produces but whom it lifts, that secure supply chains matter as much as stock indices, that productive capacity is power, and that trade policy, industrial policy, and national security policy must all fit together or they will each fail separately. So let me end where I began: with President Reagan's reminder to identify where we went wrong and to recover our faith in what can go right. If we learn from our mistakes, if we rebuild what we neglected, if we reward production as well as consumption, if we prize resilience alongside prosperity, as President Trump has been doing, then this country will not simply patch over our vulnerabilities. It will enter a new season of national strength with a more robust Main Street and industrial base than ever before. We will see safer supply chains, more secure medicines, more reliable access to critical minerals, a revived maritime capacity, a financial system aligned with a national purpose, and trading partnerships that reinforce rather than undermine our security relationships. Indeed, the strength of this Republic lies in not denying that the old consensus failed. Instead, we must... we must restore resilience where complacency took hold, rebuild capacity where dependence took root, and above all, recover the confidence to believe a nation that knows how to build, invent, produce, and lead is never destined for decline. While America slept, our vulnerabilities grew, but under President Trump's leadership, we are alert to the risks we can no longer ignore and attuned to the responsibility we can no longer defer. Under President Trump, we have awoken to morning again in America, and our best days, I believe, still lie ahead. Thank you all, I look forward to exploring these themes in further conversation with Larry. Thank you. Thank you. Thank you, everybody. Thank you, Mr. Secretary, for an excellent speech. I was just sitting there thinking, having, having worked for Reagan many, many years ago: peace through strength, peace through strength, peace through strength. It hasn't changed. It hasn't changed between those two bookends. And it's a terrific thing. Peace through strength, trust but verify, exactly. And we'll get to... I want to talk about the industrial economy because your points are very important. The America First trade policy, the economy is booming. But I, you know, must ask, as the world is waiting, I guess, for new information—the President is meeting with the Cabinet, I'm sure you're plugged into this remotely—how do you read the situation? Are we close to a deal? The last Truth Social I got said basically they're looking at the issues, but the red lines have not changed with respect to denuclearization and no enrichment and reopening the Strait of Hormuz and no money in the next 30 or 60 days. It's a little sketchy, but the whole world is watching. The markets are positively reacting to it, so I thought I'd give you a chance to clarify the situation, give us your thoughts. Yeah. So, the, the President was very clear in the Cabinet meeting what he was looking for on, on the straits: free, free and open access, as we saw before February 27th, and that Iran must give up their highly enriched uranium and cannot have a nuclear weapon. And to be clear, I, I think one of the things that has gone unappreciated and unnoticed here, this is the first time the Iranians have been willing to discuss the nuclear subject. Like, before, it was, "We have the right to do this, we have undisputed." And so, you know, I think between five and a half, six weeks of an incredibly successful military campaign, and then Operation Economic Fury, where we have really cut them off, they are at the end of their tether now financially. I think 40 or 50% of the troops aren't getting paid, police aren't reporting to the station, inflation's probably over 200%, they're having to give out food vouchers, and they, they have turned off the internet. So, there was an information vacuum. And, you know, we'll, we'll see as the, the Iranians have engaged as they could. I would point out we did not change... we did not have regime change, but we changed the regime. The, the first level of leaders is decapitated, the second level is decapitated, so we're dealing with, with the third level. And, you know, it's, it's very tough because on one side, you know, we have a theocracy with the clerics, and on the other side, we have a thugocracy with the IRGC. And, you know, you've got to convince both sides. You know, I think, I think kudos to you. You are probably the most significant, most powerful, most successful wartime Treasury Secretary, certainly since World War II. You've closed them down. They are our enemies. In fact, Roger Zakheim reminds me, the Reagan Institute did a poll: 80% of Americans, 80% of Americans believe Iran is an enemy of the United States. Now, where are the other 20...? I, I don't know who the other 20 are. They're all, they're all in New York and Connecticut. I believe that aside. But what you have done with the Economic Fury, you've basically turned their water off. Maybe you want to say, how, how long can they...? They're not meeting payrolls, they're not meeting retirement, and they're not showing up for work. Reid Hoffman on Why Everyone is Now a Manager of Agents

SAIR(601.0K) ·29:31 ·1.6K views · 2026-05-29
Reid Hoffman (Co-founder, LinkedIn and Partner, Greylock)
The rapid advancement of AI is transitioning the workforce from individual execution to agentic management, where knowledge workers must act as "managers of agents" utilizing closed-loop, compounding systems.
TL;DR The rapid advancement of AI is transitioning the workforce from individual execution to agentic management, where knowledge workers must act as "managers of agents" utilizing closed-loop, compounding systems. This cognitive industrial revolution is poised to drastically...

TL;DR

The rapid advancement of AI is transitioning the workforce from individual execution to agentic management, where knowledge workers must act as "managers of agents" utilizing closed-loop, compounding systems. This cognitive industrial revolution is poised to drastically accelerate scientific discovery, particularly in complex biological fields like oncology, provided startups can successfully bridge the gap between academic research and business value.

The Shift from Generative AI to Agentic Workflows

  • The "Manager of Agents" Paradigm: In the modern landscape of knowledge work, professionals must transition their mindset away from being solo contributors. Instead, the baseline expectation is to design, deploy, and manage agentic workflow frameworks.
      • 24/7 Productivity: Agentic workflows allow systems to run continuously without fatigue, performing brute-force exploration and complex multi-layered tasks.
      • Simulated Team Dynamics: Advanced agentic frameworks prompt a single AI model to take on distinct roles within a simulated team structure, allowing a single operator to wield the productivity of an entire department.
  • Compounding Learning Loops: The breakthrough in AI capability lies in the integration of probabilistic generative systems with deterministic code execution.
      • Automatic Refactoring: Tools like Claude Code Opus 4.6 and GPT-5.5 Codex allow a probabilistic system to write deterministic code, execute it, evaluate the output, and automatically refactor it.
      • Case Study (The *Possible* Podcast): Reid Hoffman and co-host Aria Finger streamlined their podcast translation process into 20 languages. Using GPT-5.5 Codex, they transitioned from a highly complex workflow to a simplified, one-shot multi-agent system.

AI in Biology and Science (Insights from Manas AI)

  • The Complexity of Biology: While highly complex games like Go have more potential moves than there are atoms in the universe, biology is exponentially more complex.
      • Biological "Move 37" Moments: AlphaGo's famous "Move 37" initially looked like a mistake (a hallucination) but was actually a stroke of genius. In biology, AI can predict novel molecular targets and drug bindings, but these virtual "hallucinations" must be validated through rigorous in-silico and physical laboratory testing.
  • Addressing Biological Data Scarcity: Despite the vastness of the natural world, structured and clean biological data is incredibly sparse.
      • "Sid-Reinforcement Learning": To train predictive algorithms efficiently, Manas AI uses the domain expertise of co-founder Dr. Siddhartha Mukherjee to focus computational power on the most promising atmospheric patterns of complex biological spaces.
  • Accelerating Oncology Timelines: Manas AI has already discovered several Investigational New Drug (IND) candidates through prototyping.
      • A Ten-Year Outlook: Hoffman estimates that within the next decade, AI-augmented science will deliver viable "shots on goal" to solve 10 to 30 of the major, most difficult cancers (such as blood cancers and leukemia).

Demystifying AI Hype and Navigating Public Backlash

  • Sifting Hype from Reality: While extreme claims—such as uploading human consciousness into Von Neumann probes for 15 cents within two years—are highly unrealistic, the compounding nature of exponential curves makes significant, unpredictable white-collar disruptions highly possible over the next one to five years.
  • Anticipating Negative Public Sentiment: Hoffman projects that public anxiety regarding AI will intensify through 2026 and 2027.
      • Fear of Disruption: Up to 70% of the public prefers the certainty of lacking downside over potential upside. Disruptive technologies naturally trigger resistance due to fears over job displacement and changes to the human experience.
  • The Path of "Tangible AI": To combat negative narratives, governments and organizations should focus on deploying immediately beneficial, free utilities—such as legal, educational, and medical assistants—directly to everyone's smartphones to deliver clear, everyday value.

Entrepreneurial Advice for Scientists and Raising Capital

  • The Scientist's "Business Fitness Function": Brilliant academic researchers frequently stumble in business because they attempt to win a "gold medal" for solving the hardest scientific problem rather than focusing on the simplest possible solution that holds high economic value.
  • Overcoming the "Software Blind Spot": Silicon Valley frequently avoids physical spaces like wet labs and drug manufacturing because the world of atoms slows progress down. However, the most successful deep-tech startups must blend advanced software acceleration with physical execution.
  • Navigating Capital and "Tech Transfer":
      • Multi-Round Planning: When raising initial seed capital, founders must plan two rounds ahead (Series A and B), outlining clear, evidence-based data thresholds that dictate when to pivot or shut down a project.
      • Matchmaking Talent: Academic scientists are rarely the right profile to manage a scaling business organization. Organizations like the SAIR Foundation should focus on "tech transfer" matchmaking—pairing world-class scientific minds with seasoned entrepreneurial and business operations talent.

Notable Quotes

"The difference in a hallucination and a good idea is one that proves out to be good, which, when originally looks novel and bad." — Reid Hoffman
"You don't make good futures by avoiding bad futures, you have to steer towards good futures... 'let's just all slow down' is unrealistic in human history." — Reid Hoffman
"No one should really be an individual contributor. They should be a manager of agents." — Reid Hoffman
"The business fitness-function challenge is the simplest thing that is valuable, not the gold medal for solving the hardest problem." — Reid Hoffman

Scott Bessent: Iran is at the END of its tether...

Fox Business(3.4M) ·26:27 ·94.2K views · 2026-05-29
Scott Bessent (U.S. Treasury Secretary)
Treasury Secretary Scott Bessent details the devastating impact of "Operation Economic Fury," a joint Treasury and U.S.
TL;DR Treasury Secretary Scott Bessent details the devastating impact of "Operation Economic Fury," a joint Treasury and U.S. Navy financial blockade that has pushed Iran to the brink of collapse. Meanwhile, the domestic U.S. economy remains highly resilient, supported by...

TL;DR

Treasury Secretary Scott Bessent details the devastating impact of "Operation Economic Fury," a joint Treasury and U.S. Navy financial blockade that has pushed Iran to the brink of collapse. Meanwhile, the domestic U.S. economy remains highly resilient, supported by permanent tax reforms, the rollout of wealth-building "Trump Accounts" for children, and a new era of independent Federal Reserve leadership under Chairman Kevin Warsh.

Operation Economic Fury and the Iranian Financial Blockade

  • Financial Collapse of the Regime: Iran is currently "at the end of its tether" financially due to the blockade.
      • Between 40% and 50% of Iranian military troops are currently unpaid, police are failing to report to stations, and domestic inflation is estimated to exceed 200%.
      • The regime has turned off the internet to manage an information vacuum and has resorted to distributing food vouchers to citizens.
  • Energy and Maritime Blockade: In coordination with the U.S. Navy, the U.S. has successfully blockaded Iranian ports, entirely shutting down operations at Kharg Island, Iran's primary oil export facility.
      • Iran currently holds its lowest volume of oil on the water in history, with China remaining its sole buying client.
  • Asset Seizures and Frozen Funds: The Treasury has frozen Iranian offshore bank accounts and directly seized approximately $1 billion in cryptocurrency wallets.
      • The U.S. is collaborating with European allies to seize luxury villas and real estate properties purchased by the Islamic Revolutionary Guard Corps (IRGC) using funds stolen from the Iranian population.
      • Prior to the blockade, the IRGC was estimated to be stealing $400 million to $500 million per month, distributed among just 80 regime leaders.
  • Strategic Mistakes by Iran: Iranian military strikes on GCC (Gulf Cooperation Council) infrastructure prompted previously hesitant Gulf allies to open up their banking systems. This transparency allowed the U.S. to swiftly target and freeze Iranian assets.

Domestic Economic Resilience and Supply-Side Growth

  • Robust Business Investment: Despite temporary energy price increases, non-defense capital goods and business investments are growing at a 19% annualized rate over the past three months (10.5% year-on-year).
      • Manufacturing shipments are up 12.5%, business profits are up 17%, and productivity is rising at nearly 3%.
  • Controlling Inflationary Pressure: Unit labor costs—a foundational metric for core inflation—grew by just 1.2% over the last four quarters.
  • The Power of Policy Permanence: Bessent credited the legislative passage of "one big beautiful bill" (bringing together the Tax Cuts and Jobs Act provisions) for establishing permanent tax rules. This certainty has driven investment rates not seen since the Reagan administration.
  • Purchasing Power Recovery: Bessent noted that under the previous administration, cumulative CPI inflation reached 21.5% while the "Common Man Index" rose up to 38%, resulting in a 15% to 16% loss in real purchasing power. The current administration aims to restore real wage growth as geopolitical tensions ease.

The Launch of "Trump Accounts" for Young Americans

  • Expanding Capital Ownership: Bessent characterized the newly established "Trump Accounts" as the most significant government benefit for young people since the GI Bill, designed to address the fact that 38% of Americans currently have zero exposure to the stock market.
  • Account Structure and Rules:
      • Seed Funding: Any child born during President Trump’s term will receive an immediate $1,000 seed investment from the Treasury, placed into low-cost index funds.
      • Family Contributions: Families with children under 18 can deposit up to $5,000 annually, compounding tax-free.
      • Maturity at 18: At age 18, the funds can be rolled into a traditional retirement plan or withdrawn tax-free to fund higher education, start a business, or make a down payment on a home.
  • Major Philanthropic Support: Billionaire philanthropists Michael and Susan Dell have committed $6.25 billion to the initiative, which will provide $250 deposits directly to children living in the bottom 80% of economic zip codes.
  • Educational Outreach: The website `trumpaccounts.gov` has launched with multiple learning modules designed to teach financial literacy and basic investing to families who have never participated in the equities market.

Monetary Policy and Federal Reserve Independence

  • A New Era under Kevin Warsh: Bessent expressed strong support for newly confirmed Federal Reserve Chairman Kevin Warsh, highlighting a return to institutional credibility.
  • Eliminating Forward Guidance: Bessent fully endorsed Chairman Warsh's plan to eliminate "forward guidance," aiming to reduce daily market-moving chatter from Fed governors and return the central bank to a quieter, more predictable background role.
  • Strict Independence: Bessent confirmed that President Trump is giving Chairman Warsh complete independence to run monetary policy without political pressure to adjust interest rates.
  • Redefining the "Strong Dollar": Bessent emphasized that a strong currency is not measured by daily price fluctuations, but by stable trade, tax, and energy policies that make the U.S. the safest and most lucrative destination for global capital.

Currency Redesign and the 250th Anniversary

  • Proposed $250 Bill: In celebration of America's upcoming semiquincentennial (250th anniversary), there is proposed House legislation to issue a $250 bill featuring President Trump.
  • Historical Precedents and Legality: While Treasury design mandates require currency portraits to feature deceased individuals, Bessent noted historical exceptions, such as the Calvin Coolidge coin minted for the nation's 150th anniversary during his presidency.
  • The Power of Seigniorage: Bessent explained that high demand for a commemorative $250 bill would generate significant "seigniorage"—essentially providing the U.S. government with an interest-free loan as citizens choose to collect and hold the currency rather than spend it.

Notable Quotes

"We have really cut them off, and they are at the end of their tether now financially. I think 40 or 50% of the troops aren't getting paid, police aren't reporting to the station, inflation's probably over 200%, they're having to give out food vouchers." — *Scott Bessent, U.S. Treasury Secretary*
"The reason that consumers have the income to spend at their kitchen tables, even with this temporary gasoline bump, is the success of these policies... supply-side economics is working, and the America First policies are working." — *Scott Bessent, U.S. Treasury Secretary*

They don't participate in the innovation, the dynamic U.S. economy... we're creating a whole new class of shareholders." — *Scott Bessent, U.S. Treasury Secretary*

The Rule for Picking AI Winners | The a16z Show

a16z(276.0K) ·33:10 ·6.8K views · 2026-05-29
David George (General Partner, a16z) David Clark (CIO, VenCap)
The generative AI market is driving unprecedented revenue scale and massive exit outcomes far faster than previous technology cycles, though identifying durable winners remains highly challenging due to rapid technological shifts and acute infrastructure supply constraints.
TL;DR The generative AI market is driving unprecedented revenue scale and massive exit outcomes far faster than previous technology cycles, though identifying durable winners remains highly challenging due to rapid technological shifts and acute infrastructure supply...

TL;DR

The generative AI market is driving unprecedented revenue scale and massive exit outcomes far faster than previous technology cycles, though identifying durable winners remains highly challenging due to rapid technological shifts and acute infrastructure supply constraints.

The Unprecedented Scale of Frontier AI

  • Hyper-Growth Velocity: Anthropic and OpenAI are adding more monthly revenue than tech giants Meta, Google, or Microsoft.
      • The combined revenue run rate of Anthropic and OpenAI is projected to reach $200 billion by the end of the year.
  • Low Market Diffusion: Despite massive revenue growth, the actual diffusion of AI technology into the broader enterprise economy remains under 5%, indicating immense untapped market potential.
  • Enterprise Budget Constraints: The Fortune 500 generates roughly $2 trillion in collective annual profits. A $200 billion run rate from the top two AI companies represents a significant shift, capturing about 10% of that total profit pool and forcing enterprises to find cost offsets to fund AI adoption.
  • Rapid Expansion Across Industries: AI integration is scaling beyond coding into other white-collar sectors. The legal sector, though smaller than software engineering, is seeing rapid takeoff in usage as models improve.

Shifting From Skeuomorphic to Native AI Enterprises

  • The Evolution of AI Apps: The market is transitionary. While most current enterprise AI usage is skeuomorphic (using AI to do existing tasks slightly faster), cutting-edge companies are shifting toward native agentic AI and proactive engagement models.
  • Lean Operating Models: Native AI startups are structurally different from the previous SaaS generation, which grew headcounts inefficiently. New startups operate with extreme lean-ness. Researchers and engineers frequently run swarms of automated agents using voice-prompting ("whispering" to their computers) rather than typing.
  • Resource Allocation Trends: High-performing tech startups are dedicating their best engineering talent to product innovation and creating new capabilities rather than automating internal company workflows.
  • The "Documentation Phase": Traditional enterprises trying to adopt AI are currently stuck in the "documentation phase"—converting unstructured business data into markdown files to capture context before building automated workflows.

Volatile Value Capture and the Defensibility Dilemma

  • Short Start-Up Half-Lives: Identifying durable winners is increasingly difficult. Year-over-year, 40% of the startups featured on the *Forbes AI 50* list dropped off, highlighting the incredibly short half-life of early leaders.
  • The "Token Path" Imperative: To defend their pricing and maintain budget priority among cost-conscious buyers, startups must position themselves directly in the "token path" (the direct line of computing usage and value generation).
  • Unknowable Market Structures: The distribution of economic value remains highly uncertain and depends heavily on:
      • Whether the frontier model market consolidates to a few players (higher token prices) or expands to five or more (lower token prices).
      • The viability of open-source model distillation, which currently costs only about 2% of the original pre-training cost to execute.
      • The speed of transition to smaller, localized models that run with 80% of frontier capability at 10% of the cost.

Reforming Venture Capital for Hyper-Growth

  • Exponentially Larger Exits: The threshold for a top 1% venture capital exit has escalated dramatically:
      • 2020–2024: $10 billion
      • February 2024: $20 billion
      • Present: $32 billion (anchored by companies like Wiz)
      • September 2024 (Projected): Could exceed $100 billion with anticipated listings from OpenAI and Anthropic.
  • IPO Comparison: The collective value of all venture-backed IPOs over the past six years is roughly $1 trillion. This total is expected to be smaller than the combined eventual public market value of just three top-tier private companies (SpaceX, OpenAI, and Anthropic).
  • Early-Stage Risk Tolerance: High-conviction investing in frontier tech requires VCs to accept a high loss ratio (traditionally 60% at the early stage) to ensure they back the ultimate category leaders.
  • The Demand for Platform Scale: AI companies are scaling so fast that they run into "big company" challenges (international expansion, complex cloud contracts, pricing design) while their teams are still very small. Startups like Cursor are generating massive scale with minimal headcounts, driving a preference for larger VC firms that offer scaled operating platforms.

Macro Constraints: Supply Bottlenecks vs. Bubble Risk

  • A Supply-Constrained Market: Unlike historical market bubbles characterized by oversupply, the AI sector is strictly demand-heavy and supply-constrained.
  • Data Center Bottlenecks: Physical infrastructure is severely backlogged. Large-scale data center capacity is fully booked out until late 2028 or early 2029, with US builds running approximately one year behind schedule due to component shortages and local community resistance.
  • Rationalizing CapEx: A projected $5 trillion in infrastructure CapEx over the next four to five years requires roughly $1 trillion to $2 trillion in revenue returns to justify the spend. This equation appears highly achievable if the top model companies alone are approaching $200 billion in revenue run rates.

Notable Quotes

"I feel pretty confident saying that we're not in a bubble right now... We're massively supply-constrained. You can't get data center capacity at scale until late '28, early '29 right now, and that's just a fact." — David George, General Partner at a16z

The SpaceX IPO: Science Fiction or Serious Investment? | The Weekly Wrap

Steve Eisman(175.0K) ·18:27 ·47.4K views · 2026-05-29
Steve Eisman (Host, The Weekly Wrap) Elon Musk (CEO, SpaceX)
SpaceX's upcoming IPO seeks an unprecedented $1.75 to $2 trillion valuation (~100x sales) that relies on sci-fi narrative appeal and relaxed Nasdaq index rules rather than its decelerating fundamentals.
TL;DR SpaceX's upcoming IPO seeks an unprecedented $1.75 to $2 trillion valuation (~100x sales) that relies on sci-fi narrative appeal and relaxed Nasdaq index rules rather than its decelerating fundamentals. Meanwhile, traditional tech showcases a sharp split: hardware giants...

TL;DR

SpaceX's upcoming IPO seeks an unprecedented $1.75 to $2 trillion valuation (~100x sales) that relies on sci-fi narrative appeal and relaxed Nasdaq index rules rather than its decelerating fundamentals. Meanwhile, traditional tech showcases a sharp split: hardware giants like Dell are booming on real AI infrastructure spend, while software players like Salesforce face pressure as AI disruption fears linger.

The SpaceX IPO: Sci-Fi Valuation vs. Fundamental Reality

  • The Valuation Disconnect: SpaceX is aiming for a valuation of $1.75 to $2 trillion, translating to a price-to-sales multiple of roughly 100x.
      • *Peer Comparison:* By comparison, Palantir trades at under 70x trailing sales, and Nvidia trades at a price-to-revenue ratio of only 14x despite posting 85% revenue growth.
      • *Market Cap vs. Revenue:* At $2 trillion, SpaceX would be the 7th largest company by market cap, yet its revenue ranks around 200th—on par with Kellogg's.
  • Manipulated Index Inclusion: Nasdaq has adjusted its standard rules to guarantee the IPO's success. Despite SpaceX floating only about 5% of its shares (roughly $75 billion), Nasdaq will allow it to enter major indices just 15 days after the IPO. Furthermore, Nasdaq will multiply SpaceX’s float by three times for its index weight calculations.
  • Targeting Retail Investors: Elon Musk is reserving an unprecedented 30% of the IPO allocation for retail investors. Eisman argues this is because the valuation can only be sustained if SpaceX becomes a "retail cult stock."
  • Financial Breakdown (Q1 2026):
      • *Overall Results:* SpaceX generated $4.7 billion in revenue for Q1 2026, representing 15% year-over-year growth—a stark deceleration from its 33% revenue growth in 2025. The company posted a net loss of $1.9 billion for the quarter.
      • *By Division:*
          • Connectivity (Starlink): Generated $3.3 billion in revenue (up 32% year-over-year) and is the company's only profitable business unit.
          • Space (Rocket Launches): Generated $619 million, down 28% due to launch mission timing and government contract delays.
          • AI (Grok + X): Generated $818 million but remains highly unprofitable.
      • *Exaggerated TAM:* The IPO prospectus claims a total addressable market (TAM) of $28.5 trillion. Strikingly, 93% ($26.5 trillion) of this TAM is attributed to its AI division, which is currently its smallest, most unprofitable unit and is reportedly bypassed by SpaceX's own engineers for being subpar.

Tech Earnings: Dell's Hardware Boom vs. Salesforce's Software Struggles

  • Dell Technologies' AI Capex Tailwind: Dell's stock has surged 148% year-to-date, demonstrating how heavily AI infrastructure spending is benefiting the hardware sector.
      • *Q1 Performance:* Dell reported powerful earnings with revenue up 39% and EPS up 45%, prompting management to raise guidance for both the next quarter and the full fiscal year.
  • Salesforce's Mixed Results: Salesforce beat expectations on its top and bottom lines for the quarter, but issued soft guidance that triggered negative market sentiment.
      • *The Guidance Miss:* Q2 revenue guidance of $11.27 to $11.35 billion missed Wall Street expectations of $11.36 billion. This guidance miss plays into broader market anxieties that generative AI represents a structural threat to traditional SaaS business models.
  • Critique of Stock-Based Compensation: Eisman noted that while Salesforce reported an adjusted EPS of $3.88, its GAAP EPS was a much lower $2.42 due to heavy stock-based compensation. He criticized the software industry's standard practice of excluding stock-based compensation from adjusted metrics, calling it "ridiculous."

Financial Sector Outlook: Bank of America & Credit Ratings Oligopolies

  • Bank of America (BAC) Bull Case: Eisman defended BAC against recent high-profile hedge fund exits.
      • *NIM Recovery:* BAC hurt its Net Interest Margin (NIM) during the pandemic by buying low-yield, long-dated Treasuries. However, because those bonds are now rolling off, BAC's NIM is poised to improve regardless of Federal Reserve policy.
      • *Strong ROE:* BAC boasts a 16% return on tangible common equity. With no U.S. recession on the immediate horizon to drive up credit losses, Eisman views the stock as a solid holding.
  • The Ratings Agencies (Moody's and S&P Global): Eisman remains highly bullish on both ratings giants, owning Moody's himself.
      • *Explaining the 5-Year Underperformance:* Both stocks lagged the broader market over the last five years because the zero-rate environment of 2020–2021 pulled a decade of corporate debt refinancing forward. This created an issuance drought from 2022 to 2024, which is finally ending as debt issuance picks back up.
      • *The Ethical Conundrum:* Addressing investor concerns over the agencies' role in stamping AAA ratings on toxic subprime debt prior to the 2008 Great Financial Crisis, Eisman noted that while they "sold their souls" for fees back then, both companies are now under entirely different management teams.

Notable Quotes

"The valuation is kind of crazy, and the only way to justify it is for SpaceX to become a retail cult stock. So this is an IPO of a sci-fi story tailor-made for a sci-fi cult." — Steve Eisman
"At a valuation of 1.75 to 2 trillion, SpaceX will have the seventh largest market cap. But in terms of revenue, it ranks around 200 with Kellogg's. Of course, selling Froot Loops is a mundane but profitable business." — Steve Eisman
"Personally, I think excluding stock-based comp from earnings is ridiculous." — Steve Eisman
This week's war news was very mysterious. President Trump made it sound like a settlement was close, but then it wasn't. Salesforce reported and did itself no great favors. The overall software narrative remains negative as fears about the impact of AI remain in force. SpaceX is supposed to go public on June 12th. Like Uber and Uber Eats, the cost of using AI in the form of tokens has been completely subsidized, and that is just now beginning to change. In this week's Wrap, we will discuss the war in Iran, the SpaceX IPO, two quarterly earnings reports from Salesforce and Dell, and one mailbag about investing in banks and in the ratings agencies. This week's war news was very mysterious. Over the weekend, President Trump made it sound like a settlement was close, but then it wasn't, so nobody knows where we are. Despite this fog of war, the market still sees the war glass as half full and keeps marching higher. And now, SpaceX. SpaceX is supposed to go public on June 12th, which is soon. The S-1 is out. Because this will be the biggest IPO in history, I thought it would be a good idea to analyze it. Elon Musk is certainly ambitious about his company. The very first sentence of the S-1 states, quote: "Our mission is to build the systems and technologies necessary to make life multiplanetary, to understand the true nature of the universe, and to extend the light of consciousness to the stars." That is the mission statement, and it sounds like a Star Trek episode. Now, I love Star Trek, so I'm all in favor of a Star Trek remake, but not at this valuation. By the way, the phrase "extend consciousness to the stars" appears 10 times in the document. I guess a good line bears repeating. Also, one of the potential future businesses listed in the document is, get this, asteroid mining. Why bother mining for stuff on Earth when you can mine an asteroid? Asteroid mining is a key theme of the new sci-fi show on Apple called "For All Mankind". Maybe Elon got the idea from that show, or maybe he's a writer for the show itself. If only a bank's mission statement could command these multiples. But somehow, the words "We'd like to expand our net interest margin" just don't generate the same energy and passion as multiplanetary exploration. Now, before we get to the fundamentals, we need to look at how Nasdaq stacked the deck to make sure our rocket ship of a stock has a successful launch into the stock market stratosphere. How did Nasdaq conspire with SpaceX? By changing all the rules for SpaceX to allow it to be included in indices. Even though SpaceX is only going to float around 5% of its shares, Nasdaq decided that, given the potential $1.75 to $2 trillion total valuation, a float that equates to around 75 billion needs to join the index. Now, normally, Nasdaq would not allow a 5% floating stock to enter into any indices, or at least not for many, many, many months. Also, the index weighting would be small. SpaceX, however, will be allowed to enter indices only 15 days after the IPO. Also, instead of getting a small weighting, Nasdaq will multiply SpaceX's float by three times for index calculations. Finally, Musk is reserving 30% of the offering for retail. This is unheard of for a serious company. In the usual IPO, shares are mostly allocated to institutions, and retail receives a pittance. So why do it this way? Because the valuation is kind of crazy, and the only way to justify it is for SpaceX to become a retail cult stock. So this is an IPO of a sci-fi story tailor-made for a sci-fi cult. To infinity and beyond! Or in this case, to Mars. SpaceX is composed of three divisions: the first, Space, this is the rocket launching business for satellites; two, Connectivity, this is Starlink, which provides mobile connectivity all over the globe; and three, AI, a combination of AI data centers and an LLM called Grok plus X. By the way, for those of you who wonder where the word Grok comes from, it is a word created by the science fiction author Robert Heinlein in his classic novel "Stranger in a Strange Land", which is about a messianic figure from Mars. The word "grok" means to understand something deeply and profoundly. Clearly, Elon Musk read this book and takes his science fiction very seriously. By the way, I read that novel when I was in high school. To justify the valuation, Musk and the bankers value the total addressable market, the TAM, as equaling $28.5 trillion: 370 billion for space solutions, 1.6 trillion for connectivity, and 26.5 trillion for AI. Now, 28.5 trillion is only slightly smaller than the GDP of the entire United States. Also, it's funny that 93% of the TAM is related to AI when that is now the smallest division and loses the most money. There are reports that the Space division's own engineers will not use Grok at all because it is a subpar product. But this is pretty bold. Then again, this is the first time a company has a TAM of the entire universe, so who's to say? But let's take a look at the actual current numbers which are, to put it frankly, not so bold. In 1Q26, SpaceX generated total revenue of 4.7 billion, representing 15% total revenue growth, which is a deceleration from the 33% revenue growth in all of 2025. Still, 15% revenue growth isn't bad. But as we shall see, it is not so great against peers. The company generated a loss of 1.9 billion in 1Q26, and here's how the three divisions did. Of the total 4.7 billion in 1Q26, 619 million of revenue was in Space, 3.3 billion from Connectivity, and 818 million from AI. Only Connectivity generated an operating profit. Perhaps the most important thing to notice is that the Space division's revenue was down 28% because of, quote, "lower launch service missions and the timing of work for government contracts," end quote. But then again, interplanetary work takes time. Space may be an exciting business, but any business that can experience a decline in revenue of 28% has issues. It seems to me that SpaceX has only one great business: Connectivity. Connectivity had 1Q26 revenue growth of 32%. The space business can be erratic, and AI is anyone's guess given increasing competition and a potential race to the bottom on the pricing of tokens. The valuation Musk is looking for is almost 100 times sales. Now, Palantir currently has the highest trailing price-to-sales multiple at slightly less than 70 times trailing sales, and around 45 times 2026 estimated sales. More importantly, let's stack up SpaceX's 1Q revenue growth against its trillion-dollar market cap brethren. As seen on this table, the six companies with the largest market caps, Nvidia, Google, Apple, Microsoft, Amazon, and Broadcom—the Big Six—all had faster revenue growth than SpaceX's 15% for 1Q26. The Big Six had revenue growth of 16% to 85%. Nvidia posted 85% revenue growth but has a price-to-revenue valuation of only 14 times, as compared to the 100 times Musk is shooting for. The highest price-to-revenue ratio in the trillionaire club is Broadcom at 19 times. SpaceX as a story sounds great, but the numbers are just not that impressive. But science fiction always requires suspension of disbelief. Why get in the way of a good story? That is why the mission statement sounds like an intro to a Star Trek episode, and why the company's so-called TAM is an insane number. How else can you justify a valuation of 100 times revenue? You need poetry, or to have been raised on a steady diet of sci-fi and Star Trek. Do you grok it? One additional point: at a valuation of 1.75 to 2 trillion, SpaceX will have the seventh largest market cap. But in terms of revenue, it ranks around 200, alongside Kellogg's. Of course, selling Froot Loops is a mundane but profitable business. One more thing on SpaceX: there are rumors that Musk wants to merge Tesla with SpaceX after it goes public. If that were to happen, the combined company would be unbelievably complex. And now for two earnings reports. Salesforce reported and did itself no great favors. The overall software narrative remains negative as fears about the impact of AI remain in force. While there has been something of a software rally of late, I think it's very tenuous. On the positive side, adjusted EPS was 3.88 versus 3.12 expected and versus 2.58 last year, so a nice beat. However, let me point out that Salesforce's adjusted EPS, like other software companies, excludes stock-based comp. GAAP EPS was a more mundane $2.42 versus the adjusted 3.88 figure. A lot of stock-based comp is in there. Personally, I think excluding stock-based comp from earnings is ridiculous. As for revenue, 11.13 billion beat the expected 11.05 billion and was up 13% versus last year. So far, so good. The guidance was not so good. Salesforce guided adjusted EPS to 3.25 to 3.27 versus 3.25 expected, so just in line. Revenue was guided to 11.27 to 11.35 billion, but expectations are for 11.36 billion, so a miss. Given the horrendous narrative surrounding software, the guidance miss on revenue is not good, and the stock was down a bit after hours. Not a disaster, but not great either. Nevertheless, Salesforce was up on Thursday because Snowflake reported great numbers, and that gave a lift to the entire software group. Dell, a hardware company, has been on a tear this year with the stock up 50% just this month and up 148% this year. Dell's numbers show how much AI capex is impacting the entire hardware tech sector. Dell reported powerful numbers, with both revenue and EPS beating expectations. Quarterly revenue was up an impressive 39%, and EPS was up 45%. It raised guidance for the next quarter and for the fiscal year. And now for the mailbag. We have one mailbag this week, and it's from Maria. Now, Maria's question was quite long, so I've edited it down pretty considerably. It's still long, though. "Hi, Steve. I've been tracking," and she mentions a particular hedge fund manager, "1Q2026 13F. He, the hedge fund manager, trimmed his Bank of America position by over 70% and opened equal-sized new stakes in S&P Global and Moody's. One reading of the BAC exit is that large-cap banking is a low-margin, capital-heavy business sitting at the end of a rate cycle. BAC's net interest margin runs at 2.01 to 2.45%, which is thin and likely peaked." "Banks must hold enormous regulatory capital against every dollar of loans, which structurally caps return on equity at 12 to 15%. If the rate tailwind that lifted NIM over the past few years is now fading and credit quality begins to soften into a slower economy, you are left holding the most cyclically exposed part of the financial system at what looks like a full valuation." "Then she turns to the ratings agencies. 'Here is a paradox I keep coming back to and I would genuinely value your take on it. If the volume of global debt has grown so dramatically over the last several years, the ratings agencies should have seen a corresponding structural lift in their earnings and stock prices. They haven't—not over the last 5 years.'" "S&P's 5-year total return is roughly 24% against a broader market that approximately doubled. The explanation I've pieced together is that 2020 and '21 were extraordinary issuance years driven by near-zero rates. Companies essentially pulled a decade of refinancing forward into 2 years, leaving an issuance drought from 2022 through 2024. At the same time, both stocks experienced significant multiple compression." "The ethical question I'm generally torn on, and I'll be direct about something that gives me pause: investing in Moody's or S&P requires me to get comfortable with their role in 2008. I know they stamped AAA on instruments that deserved junk status. What I don't fully understand is the allocation of responsibility. Were the agencies the primary driver or were they captured by the investment banks structuring the products?" "I'm not looking for absolution, but I think there is a meaningful difference between a structurally captured institution and a predatory one. If you have a view on where the agencies actually sat in that chain of culpability, it would genuinely help me decide." There's a lot here, so let's take them one at a time. First, with respect to the banks in general, and Bank of America in particular. It's true that they are capital-intensive businesses and the regulators restrict their leverage. That has always been true. Bank of America's return on tangible common equity is actually 16%, which is quite good given how much the banks have been forced to delever. It's also true that banks never do well in a recession because credit costs climb. However, for now, I just don't see a recession on the horizon. With respect to the company's net interest margin, Bank of America made a mistake during COVID and buried itself in long-dated Treasuries. That move has hurt its NIM, but those bonds are rolling off, so the net interest margin should improve over the next several years, regardless of Fed policy. I think it's okay to own Bank of America. With respect to the ratings agencies, I like both of them because they are oligopolies. The stocks have underperformed because debt issuance exploded during COVID as rates went to zero. Issuance has been light for the last several years, but that is now coming to an end, and issuance is picking up. I like both companies, and I own Moody's myself. As for your ethical conundrum, it's true that the ratings agencies did bad things leading up to the GFC. They were paid three to four times more to rate subprime ABS and CDOs, so they sold their souls to make the extra fees. However, today they are owned by different management teams, so I think you don't have to agonize over owning these stocks.
