🎙️ Inside the Next Financial Crisis with Lloyd Blankfein #podcast #theforum

The Economic Club of New York(11.7K) ·24:14 ·3.1K views · 2026-05-29
Lloyd Blankfein (Former Chairman and CEO, Goldman Sachs) Becky Quick (Anchor, CNBC)
Lloyd Blankfein warns that a 15-year absence of a major financial reckoning has allowed overvalued, mismarked private assets to accumulate like "dry tinder" awaiting a spark.
TL;DR Lloyd Blankfein warns that a 15-year absence of a major financial reckoning has allowed overvalued, mismarked private assets to accumulate like "dry tinder" awaiting a spark. However, he notes that the US economy remains highly resilient due to its flexible labor market,...

TL;DR

Lloyd Blankfein warns that a 15-year absence of a major financial reckoning has allowed overvalued, mismarked private assets to accumulate like "dry tinder" awaiting a spark. However, he notes that the US economy remains highly resilient due to its flexible labor market, and the Federal Reserve possesses stronger tools to manage a downturn today than it did in 2008.

Market Cycles, Animal Spirits, and the "Dry Tinder" Risk

  • The Inevitability of Financial Cycles: Market cycles are driven by the natural ebbing of traumatic memories. As the memory of the 2008 financial crisis fades, a new generation of market participants has become "sporty," taking on more risk and sowing the seeds for the next reckoning.
  • The "Forest Floor" Asset Risk: The primary risk in today's economy is not a specific catalyst, but rather the heavy accumulation of mismarked private assets on corporate balance sheets.
      • Private equity funds and insurance companies are holding onto aging assets despite record-high equity markets, indicating these assets are likely marked too high to attract buyers.
      • Blankfein compares this to dry timber on a forest floor. The eventual spark—whether a fat-finger error, a bad credit event, or a geopolitical shock—is inevitable and less important than the sheer volume of fuel waiting to burn.
  • The Need for Preventive "Brush Fires": Small, controlled market adjustments are healthy. While private credit is currently experiencing a mild, positive burnoff, the lack of a broader systemic reckoning over the last 15 years has prevented the natural clearing of bad assets from balance sheets.

The Shift to Private Credit and Structural Regulation Challenges

  • The Rise of Regulatory Arbitrage: Capital naturally migrates to areas with the fewest regulatory capital requirements, which has fueled the massive expansion of the unregulated private credit market.
  • The Fed's Coordination Problem: In 2008, the Federal Reserve could manage a systemic crisis by gathering representatives from just 5 to 16 major banks that held 75% to 90% of outstanding loans.
      • Today, due to the highly distributed nature of private credit, the Fed lacks both visibility and direct influence.
      • Blankfein notes that coordinating a similar workout today would require a venue the size of "Citi Field" rather than a single boardroom.
  • Secondary Market Pressure: Because private sponsors are delaying asset sales, limited partners (such as university endowments) are not receiving cash distributions. This has forced LPs to sell their limited partnership interests at a discount in secondary markets to raise capital.

Federal Reserve Tools and the National Debt Outlook

  • Stronger Crisis Defensive Tools: If a crisis occurs today, the US is in a much better position to respond than in 2008:
      • Banking Stability: Unlike the Great Financial Crisis, today's banking system is flush with capital and not in distress.
      • Rate Flexibility: With interest rates sitting between 3.75% and 4%, the Fed has significant room to cut rates, unlike the zero-rate environment of the post-2008 era.
  • How the US "Defaults" via Inflation: Because the US borrows in its own currency, it will not default nominally. Instead, default occurs when the government pays back creditors with devalued dollars that have lost their purchasing power.
  • The Importance of Fed Independence: The Federal Reserve is the ultimate institution defending the purchasing power of the dollar. Political assaults on Fed independence signal to global creditors that their investments are unsafe, which could trigger a sudden refusal to finance US debt or demand significantly higher interest rates.

US vs. European Economic Resilience and Social Contracts

  • Flexible Labor Markets Drive US Growth: The US economy's rapid recovery post-2008—contrasted with Europe's stagnant recovery—is a direct result of different national social contracts.
      • In the US, companies can rapidly downsize and restructure (e.g., Meta cutting its workforce by 8%), allowing labor and capital to be quickly recycled into more productive, high-growth industries.
  • European "Zombie" Companies: Europe’s social contract heavily protects existing jobs, making it incredibly difficult to lay off workers.
      • While this limits short-term unemployment shocks, it creates "zombie" companies, leaves mismarked assets on balance sheets, and ultimately leads to lower long-term growth and higher structural unemployment.
      • Though the US model leads to greater political polarization and social friction, it fosters unmatched innovation, resilience, and economic speed.

Notable Quotes

"People with P&Ls can't walk away from their... you know, can't deny what they had thought in the past. And so, I think we have a system that allows for animal spirits, and with that comes the inevitability... I wish it would never go off the rails. But if you're going to have animal spirits... that's going to produce a kind of cycle." — Lloyd Blankfein
"I'm more worried about the kindling than I am about the cigarette, you know, the loose cigarette butt at this point. That's what I would work on if I were, you know, a regulator today. I'd be worried about getting rid of all the mismarked assets here." — Lloyd Blankfein
"How does a country default when it borrows money in its own currency? The safety of that... that our creditors look to to defend the purchasing power of the dollar, is the Fed. And so, this kind of an assault on the independence of the Fed, that just makes our creditors think that they're going to get defaulted to." — Lloyd Blankfein
I'm Becky Quick of CNBC and your host of The Forum. I'll be guiding you through exclusive conversations among some of the world's global leaders. Conversations previously held behind closed doors, but today, we invite you in. The Economic Club of New York serves as the premier forum for nonpartisan discussion, dedicated to connecting the world's brightest minds with preeminent public and private sector leaders. A nonprofit, 501(c)(3), the club is a 115-year-old platform for the conversations that help shape the future of our world. The Economic Club of New York: Brightest Minds, Critical Conversations, Catalyst for Innovation. If you had to do it all over again and change something about not just the culture of Goldman, but the culture of Wall Street, what would it be? Well, you know, I'm a markets guy, so I'm more in the responder net to what happens. I take certain things... you know, I think things fall the way they are for a reason. What would I like? I'd like the sun to shine every day and the birds to chirp every day. If I were in charge, I'd make sure that that happened. But I do think that Wall Street does a very good job of responding to the uncertainties that exist at every moment about what is going to happen next, being good contingency planners, being very resilient, and being great responders. Of course, they get killed by the reviewers and the pundits who have after-acquired information to look back and say, "Aha, that's not what I would have done." Just like nobody voted for Nixon, except he won in a landslide, everybody always remembers being right because they're pundits, and pundits don't have P&Ls. People with P&Ls can't walk away from... you know, they can't deny what they had thought in the past. And so, I think we have a system that allows for animal spirits, and with that comes the inevitability. You ask me what I wish—I wish it would never go off the rails. But if you're going to have animal spirits, that is going to produce a kind of cycle where you have a bad outcome. Then, everybody gets nervous about risk, husbanding capital, and being afraid to take risk. Over time, the memory of the trauma of the reckoning, of the crisis, ebbs, and people get a little bit more sporty, which is what is happening today. With dimmer memories, people retiring, and new people coming in who only read about the financial crisis or the tech bubble in books, it doesn't quite register. People get sportier and sportier, which then sows the seeds for the next crisis and the next reckoning. In that case, everybody tightens up, bad assets get off balance sheets, things get shipshape again, and it takes another couple of years for that to go. And guess what? That's why we have a business cycle, and that's why we have historical cycles. Can you imagine if we all lived forever and we kept our memories intact forever? We'd be afraid to do anything. So, it's no secret you've been quite critical about the shift of activity since 2008 into private markets. No, no, I'm an observer of it; I'm not critical. By the way, Goldman Sachs was a participant in private markets. Not everything we did was in the regulated part of the firm. We had funds that were unregulated private credit funds; not everything was done on balance sheet. So, I'm not critical. I'm an observer of it, and I've commented on what the risks are that people are underestimating. So, what do you think is the biggest risk, and do you think it's a big enough risk to face another systemic issue as we did in 2008? Look, let me be clear, going back to what I just said about cycles. In this kind of cycle, when you start feeling good and sprightly, you get less disciplined about it. As we separate ourselves from the trauma—and we haven't really had one for more than 15 years since the Great Financial Crisis—things get a little more lax. It's just, again, the nature of things. And the real issue for me in the market today is that we haven't had a reckoning in a long time, so we haven't cleared those assets that are on balance sheets that probably are marked too high. People thought they were worth X, and they may not be worth X. Why do I say that? Because a lot of these private assets were acquired on the balance sheets of private equity funds, insurance companies bought them, and the companies that do this activity are in business to recycle them and sell them as soon as they can. We've just had the highest equity markets ever and the best financing markets ever, yet assets are getting kind of aged on balance sheets. So that must mean that people who are highly motivated to sell them are not selling them, because the price isn't being met, probably because they're marked too high. So, the metaphor that I would use is that we've been accumulating a lot of dry tinder on the floor of the forest. If you think of California and when it gets dry, they put out the fire alerts because it's dry outside. The real problem is the fuel on the floor of the forest: the tinder, the dried wood that fell down. And at some point, inevitably, someone will toss a cigarette butt, someone will be careless with a campfire, or there'll be a lightning strike. Do you blame the lightning, or do you blame the cigarette? It's inevitable. It's really the accumulation of the debris, the fuel, that makes this inevitable at some point. I think, because we have not had a reckoning, we've not had those preventive brush fires that they try to do in a controlled way to burn that stuff off. We are having one now, a little bit, in private credit, which is a good thing. You want these little things; markets pay attention to little things and get them out of the way before they engulf and get out of control. That's a little bit like a controlled burnoff. But because we haven't had that in a big sense in a long time, I'm nervous it will be something. It could be a fat finger. It could be somebody saying, "Oh my god, the price of oil is going to be sustained this high." It could be a lightning strike, or it could be a meteor hitting the planet. Something will happen that, at a different time, would not have set anything off because there wouldn't have been a lot to set off. But because that kindling is there, something like that will happen. It could be a credit event, like some highly leveraged fund being unable to make a payment. It could be an insurance regulator that looks at the balance sheet. Don't forget, insurance is regulated by 50 state regulators, not necessarily all of the same caliber. They may look at the balance sheet of an insurance company and say, "Gee, we don't know if these assets are going to be sustained. We're going to make you sell some." Then they might find themselves selling into a market that doesn't want them and won't pay the price, and that might make everybody crazy at the same time, trying to go for a door to sell stuff that no one wants. I've lived through a lot of these. It could be almost anything, and it could be something that at a different time wouldn't have registered. The problem is the accumulation of assets on balance sheets that may not be worth what they are marked. So, I have some specific questions about this because I think the post-2008 financial industry is really misunderstood. If you were a betting man, would you... I am a betting man. You know, would you believe that the private credit system coming out of this moment—if you do see the tinder starting to erase some of the froth—do you think it becomes a much bigger system coming out of this, or does it plateau? Well, Goldman Sachs, prior to 2008, was not regulated as a bank holding company. There's always regulatory arbitrage, just like there's any other arbitrage in the market. Capital will find places where it is cheapest. Capital is going to be cheap where there are fewer regulatory requirements that you hold capital against leverage, and so there'll always be stuff like this. As I said, even Goldman Sachs, in its regulated form, has funds that are private credit funds because it's other people's money in an account not subject to regulation, not on the balance sheet of the firm. In fact, ours were very big, very early. So, I think that that's a part of the market. It creates a problem in the system. Again, it might not be the spark, but it's part of the potential. In the financial crisis, the Fed could get probably 90% of all the outstanding loans around its conference room at the Federal Reserve building if they just had 16 banks represented. And by the way, if they had five represented, they could have had 75% of all the loans that were outstanding, getting together to say, "Okay, show of hands, who has this, who has that, what are we going to do about it?" Today, because of the distribution and the wider net, they'd have to take out Citi Field and have a meeting there, standing on second base to say, "Show of hands, who has this..." They don't have the visibility on it, and they don't have the influence on it. And so, I think the conditions are such that it would be hard to work out those problems. That said, under normal times, I don't think private credit is by itself necessarily such a systemic, overwhelming thing. It could be the last straw on a market that's already waiting for something to set it off, so it could have that effect. But I don't think private credit by itself is that substantial or that bad. So, I wouldn't be worried about that. Again, I'm more worried about the kindling than I am about the loose cigarette butt at this point. That's what I would work on if I were a regulator today. I'd be worried about getting rid of all the mismarked assets here; I'd be pressing. Look what's happening now in the market. Some of the sponsors aren't selling their investments. The limited partners are selling their interests in their limited partnerships at a discount because they need to raise the money. Endowments are going to the market, which is why we see the rise of all these secondary funds buying limited partnership interests; they're not getting distributions, so they have to sell. I don't want to be picking on that, as there are other things as well, and I don't think that that is hypercritical in and of itself. I think the general background is critical, ironically, in part because we haven't had a crisis in a long time. Every guy quotes from *The Godfather*, so forgive me. Remember that scene in *The Godfather* where they are talking about an impending mob war, and one of the old capos puts his arm on Michael's head and says, "Don't worry about it. We have to have these wars every ten years or so to get rid of all the grudges and bad feelings that build up." I think we have to have a crisis, unfortunately. It's the crisis that pops up every so often that acts as a reckoning for all the assets where there's no imposed discipline on getting them properly marked and distributed away from balance sheets. Would you like to be a part of the conversations at the Economic Club of New York? Learn more about membership, the New York City and National Fellows Programs, and other opportunities for engagement in the club at www.econclubny.org. So, moving on to another echo of 2008, some would argue that when you look at the Fed's balance sheet, the expansion really took off in the wake of 2008 and then really compounded in the wake of COVID as well. There was a famous op-ed in 2014, "The Asset-Rich, Income-Poor Economy," written by Kevin Warsh and Stanley Druckenmiller. Do you agree that that crisis-era tool has compounded inequality... Do I have to read that op-ed now, or... The idea stands, right? What's the... what's the question? Sorry. The idea here is that by using these tools to expand the Fed's balance sheet, we've helped asset owners—in terms of people who own stocks—rather than the small business owner who is... Well, the Fed balance sheet expanded to get long rates down. The Fed has monetary policy that affects short-term rates, but the long-term rates do what they want. You can affect short-term rates, and it won't necessarily have the same effect on long rates. Sometimes the people who buy our bonds think that what the Fed is doing in the short term to lower rates will be inflationary, so our long-term lenders demand higher interest rates, not lower interest rates, even as short rates come down. So, I know why the Fed balance sheet was used as one of the tools to stimulate the economy. By the way, one of the good things—because I said all the things I'm worried about—one of the good things today, if we had a problem, is that it would not necessarily be accompanied by a banking crisis. In the '08 situation, we had a recession, but we also had a banking crisis. That was really bad because, at the end of the day, the government doesn't lend money to people; the Fed doesn't lend. It's the banking system that does. If the banks are in distress, the money they get is husbanded and goes to refilling their coffers and reserves, and it doesn't do anything for the economy for a long time as they replenish those reserves. Today, banks are pretty much flush; it's not a bad situation. And other things are going on: interest rates are close to 3.75% or 4%, and they're not zero, so rates can be taken down. The Fed's balance sheet, while high, is lower than it has been—it's been taken down. That's another thing that could be done; the Fed could also stimulate part of the curve where people actually really do the borrowing, the longer end. And so, the tools for sorting out a problem are much more available today than they have been for a long time. So, that's the other... again, two things you have to worry about: how to avoid the problem, and having the instruments and the awareness to deal with the problem when it arises. I think the latter we're in better shape for. The real reason I ask this too is because we have a new Fed Chair coming to office who could make a lot of potentially large changes. You think about the effort to potentially reduce the balance sheet over time; do you think that he could do so without creating a taper tantrum? Well, you're talking about the Fed balance sheet; you're not talking about the balance sheet of the United States. We can talk about that next. Yeah, no, he'll... yes, he'll, you know, by the way, he's new, but he's not that new. He's been around for a long time; he was on the board before, so it's not like a pig in a poke. He's been around. I think the Fed's balance sheet is just a matter of tampering with the yield curve. And by the way, right now, people are financing the United States. Everybody's concerned, appropriately, with the size of our national debt, which keeps getting increased by our growing—not contracting—deficit, and that's creating a lot of anxiety. That situation, I'd best describe by... I think it was Hemingway who said, "How did you go bankrupt?" and the answer was, "Slowly, and then all at once." Right now we're in the slowly part of it because, in fact, we're getting financed easily. There's no... the dollar is the reserve currency. You worry about the "all at once" part, where people stop financing it because they legitimately worry that the US can default. How does the US default on its debt? We borrow in dollars and we print dollars, so how can you default? You're always going to have dollars to pay back. But what will those dollars be worth? So, the way the US defaults on its debt is it borrows dollars now, and after 10 years, we return dollars that don't have the purchasing power that they had at the beginning of the time. That's how a country defaults when it borrows money in its own currency. The safety of that—the institution that our creditors look to to defend the purchasing power of the dollar—is the Fed. And so, this kind of assault on the independence of the Fed just makes our creditors think that they're going to get defaulted on. It's like when you lend to your brother-in-law and wonder if he's ever going to pay you back; you're either not going to lend to him, or you're going to demand a very high interest rate if you do. And so, that's what everybody should be afraid of. You really want the Fed standing there as a bulwark, with nothing that undermines the idea in the minds of our creditors that the Fed won't protect the purchasing power of the dollar. Are you surprised at all by how resilient the markets have been in the face of Brent at roughly $115 a barrel, heading into now nearly the tenth... I'm... yes, fully yes. And it's not just the price of oil. If you just think of all the stuff that's been thrown at it, you have to start by accepting the resilience and work backwards from there, and say: what is it about the US? Because the US has demonstrated its resilience over and over again. You don't have to look at the current environment. You can go to the aftermath of the financial crisis, another time when the United States was ground zero for the problem, and yet the economy recovered more completely and faster. I would say the Europeans have never recovered from that crisis, yet at ground zero we have. Again, you could go back and say, in this country, you can easily... it's a different social contract. Here, you can fire people if you want to reshape your business. We have labor as almost a commodity here; people can adjust their workforce. Today, Meta, which is not doing so badly, contracts its workforce by 8%. Europe can't do it. It's a different social contract, and they would look in horror at what companies are allowed to do here. There, they would protect the workforce. Sometimes it takes years to be able to fire someone, let alone the public relations disaster it would be if a company suggested that it was going to fire workers. As a result, they have more people on payrolls and less unemployment than they otherwise would, but over the long term, more unemployment because their companies are less resilient. They have more zombie companies, more assets on balance sheets that really should be sold, terminated, or recycled into other, more productive activities, like we would do here. They would look at America and say, "Look how they are willing to immiserate their workers; they'll fire these people without any regard for what they're going to do over the years." Here, we're focused on the fact that two years later, we hire those people back into a much better business that has a chance to grow a lot better than the zombie business they were laid off from. Tell us how you really feel about Europe. No, no, I'm trying to say that people can look at these things in different ways. I lived there. They would reflect on the social contract in the US, horrified that we would so easily lay off people and not put the burden on companies to keep them on the payroll or train them for jobs that they're not necessarily suited for, rather than hire new people who could go to work the next day. They look at the way we do that and are just as legitimate in their own environment and culture, taking that point of view. I do point out, though, that ours probably leads to more polarization, disunity, and the kind of show trials and hearings you see in Washington, which gets reflected in our national politics. But it also leads to a much higher growth rate. Usually, you associate size with sloth and an inability to react and move quickly. We're the biggest economy, yet we move the fastest, we're the most resilient, and we're the most likely to foster innovation. Theirs is kind of stickier. In the short term, they probably have less unemployment than they otherwise would, but over time, they have less efficient, worse-performing businesses, and therefore lower growth and lower employment over the long run. What do you choose?
